sorry.

Made From Scratch

Chapter Summary

A vow. A wail. A promise. A sacrifice.

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

“We should be married.” Agatha’s words cut through the stillness of the night. She felt Rio stiffen next to her.

Rio’s eyes darted to Agatha as a deeply confused look spread across her face. “What’s that now sweetheart?”

“You heard me,” Agatha huffed as if she couldn’t be bothered to explain more.

Next to her in bed, Rio sat up, the sheets falling to pool at her waist as she turned and looked at Agatha. “What do you mean, we should be married?” Her brow furrowed slightly. She wasn’t sure if Agatha was serious or if this was just some idle thought.

Agatha shot her an annoyed look in return, “What do you mean, what do I mean?”

“I mean, why do you think we should define our relationship by mortal standards when you and I both know that what we have is so much more than that?”

Agatha was touched by the pure sweetness of Rio’s words. Of course , this wasn’t something Rio had even considered. She rolled her eyes at herself. Pregnancy was not suiting her. It was reawakening all the anxious parts of her that made her question if she was deserving of any of this. She just had so much love to carry. Realizing her sharpness, and taking in her love’s confused face, she grumbled and snuggled closer. She rest her head in Rio’s lap and roughly pulled her hand to her hair. A command and a plea for Rio to soothe her insecurities.

Rio understood and scratched gently at her temples, weaving her hands through her hair. Trying to defuse the tension, she took a chance with a gentle tease, “If you’re worried about what people will say, my love, I’m pretty sure Death’s child will be seen as a bastard whether he’s born out of wedlock or not.” 

The woman in her lap huffed but did not pull away. “That’s not funny.”

Rio shook her head fondly, hand still playing in Agatha’s hair. She loved this woman so much. When she was fierce. When she was insecure. When she was cocky, self-righteous, and vicious. When she was doting, soft, and serene. She sighed softly. She was extremely reluctant to make any more tenuous promises but also was unable to ever deny the woman she held anything.

“Okay, Agatha. So are you asking, or are you telling?”

Agatha turned and looked up at her, one eyebrow raised.

She rephrased her question, “Are you asking me to marry you, or are you telling me you want to be married,” she said softly. She smiled gently at her. She didn’t need a man-made title to define their relationship. But if it would make Agatha happy she would do so gladly. 

Agatha grumbled again and shifted slightly. She wouldn’t be so moody if she wasn’t so damn uncomfortable all the time.

“I don’t know. It’s silly. Just forget it––” Her deflection was stopped as Rio cupped her cheek, shaking her head slightly.

“Don’t do that. If it’s important to you, it isn’t silly.” Rio stroked her thumb over Agatha’s jaw. “If you want to be my wife, then I’ll be yours,” she promised gently.

“Are you asking me or telling me?” Agatha teased back, always clinging to a bit of petulance.

Rio rolled her eyes and lifted Agatha’s hand to her mouth, kissing her finger tenderly. She had done this once before and a daisy chain ring had blossomed. A sweet promise. This time, as her lips drew back, Agatha watched as a band of dark ebony wood curled its way around her finger.

“Will you?” Rio asked slyly.

Agatha took their joined hands and mirrored Rio, pressing a kiss to her hand in the same place. Here, two sprigs of yew intertwined and formed a braided band.

“Will you?” she breathed, tears welling slightly at the tender moment. Perhaps Rio had done this to humor her. But when she looked into her lover’s eyes she knew without a doubt that she was just as affected by this moment.

“I will.”

“Me too.”

With Rio’s help, Agatha sat up in bed, turning to kiss her. The kiss was perhaps the softest that they had ever shared.

After a moment, Rio looked down at their joined hands. A teasing smile quirked her lips before she turned to Agatha and said in mock seriousness, “You know I prefer a thinner band.”

Agatha laughed and rolled her eyes, kissing her love, her wife , again just to shut up her teasing. “Never mind, I change my mind. I can’t stand you,” she shot back, still beaming.

“No way. No take backs. Till death do us part,” Rio teased, tickling her side gently. “And I say no.”

They fell back together, Agatha’s head resting on her chest. Both of Rio’s arms wrapped around her like a shield. 

Agatha fell asleep quickly, still smiling. She wasn’t awake long enough to see her wife’s face fall with the added weight of this new promise.

As she watched Agatha drift back into sleep, a soft smile still gracing her lips, Rio held her close, her fingers absently tracing patterns along Agatha's arm. The quiet settled around them like a blanket, the only sounds in the room the slow, even breaths of her now-wife and the faint crackle of the fire. Her gaze lingered on the wooden ring around Agatha’s finger, an unbreakable circle—quite unlike the promise she’d just made. One she feared would break under the weight of fate.

The Fates’ warning echoed in her mind, each word carving itself deeper into her thoughts. "A mother’s greatest treason." She shivered, tightening her hold on Agatha as if she could keep her there forever. What kind of mother would she be to break her vows for the child she hadn’t even met yet? And yet, Rio’s loyalty, her love, was rooted so deeply in Agatha that it felt like a piece of her own soul. She would do anything to protect her.

And that was the promise she could never tell Agatha.

Lifting her head, Rio pressed a feather-light kiss to Agatha’s hair. In her sleep, Agatha murmured softly, nestling closer, as if sensing Rio’s touch even in her dreams. The sight of her, so peaceful and trusting, eased some of the weight in Rio's heart but only for a moment.

Closing her eyes, Rio whispered words only the night would hear, a private vow to the woman in her arms and the child in her womb: "I’ll give you all I can, for as long as I can. And I swear—I’ll make sure our child will know, even if he only has you, that he is loved."

As Agatha sighed in her sleep, Rio pulled her closer. She needed to leave soon. This was too much. It wasn’t enough. For the first time in a long time, she felt the familiar dread of a ticking clock.

It wouldn’t be long now.

It would be any day now. Agatha felt this with certainty. 

Tomorrow, the next, perhaps even today. Their child would be here soon. She was excited. She was terrified. She was more than a little annoyed at her wife.

Rio hadn’t been around in almost a week. It worried her. She understood that she’d probably been incredibly busy. The prolonged summer of this year had brought wildfire and drought. Farmers had scrambled to pick overripe fruit before it was scorched under the heat of the sun. She knew that was because of her, because of them.

But nevertheless, the thought of bringing this child into the world alone scared her. She stayed close to the cabin, not daring to venture deeper into the woods no matter how badly she wished to find Rio there. She had to be patient. Rio would come.

There wasn’t a world in which she would miss the birth of their child.

Agatha tried to keep her mind occupied, pacing the small cabin and smoothing the folds of the old quilt Rio had brought for their child. She hadn’t thought too hard about where she might have found it. She trailed her fingers along the window frame, eyes drifting to the woods. She imagined Rio moving through the trees, a figure swathed in shadows and sunlight, her cool presence bringing an air of calm and control that Agatha desperately needed.

But the worry gnawed at her. Each passing hour, each creak of the floorboards, each whisper of wind made her heart race with a hope she tried not to let slip into doubt.

The prolonged summer had begun to feel like an omen. She had thought it was a blessing—a chance for their child to be born under a gentle sky, in a world that felt full of warmth and life. But as the days grew hotter and the wildfires spread, Agatha couldn’t ignore the cost. She knew the land was suffering, stretching itself thin to accommodate this unnatural warmth, and she couldn't shake the feeling that something might break if it went on much longer.

The trees outside seemed to pulse with her thoughts, the leaves still green but curled, like they were bracing themselves against an inevitable end.

She was trying to convince herself, telling herself Rio was fine, that this absence was just a part of their unusual life. Yet, in moments of silence, her mind whispered doubts—what if something had happened? What if Rio was being held back, her powers split too thin as the seasons strained and cracked? The Fates had warned of treachery; was this Rio’s way of shielding her?

No, she thought stubbornly, her hands protectively covering her belly. Rio would come. She would keep her promise. She had to be patient. Rio would come. They would welcome this child as they had created him, together.

Agatha’s eyes drifted to the edge of the forest, where the shadows thickened and tangled among the trees. She felt a pang of longing. She ached for the steadying weight of her presence, the sense of protection she could offer with just a glance, especially now, with her every nerve fraying as she awaited the child she could already feel shifting restlessly within her.

She held her belly, murmuring softly to the life stirring under her palm. “Soon,” she whispered, more for herself than for him. “Your mother will be here soon.”

Agatha forced herself to sit, to keep her breaths slow and even, but as another hour slipped by, her patience wore thin. Every little noise from the woods outside made her heart leap with hope, only for it to plummet when nothing followed. The cabin felt smaller by the minute, the walls pressing in with the weight of her anticipation and growing fear. She wanted to call for Rio, to reach out with every ounce of magic she possessed and demand her presence.

She would’ve lashed out with her magic and delivered a hundred souls if it meant she would see her love walk through the door.

As the sun began to dip low in the sky, Agatha’s anxiety shifted to exhaustion, a deep weariness settling into her bones. She lit a single candle and watched its soft glow spread across the room, casting a gentle warmth over the bed and the small cradle she had prepared. She was so tired, but her mind wouldn’t let her rest. She felt another pang of a contraction that left her breathless and gasping. Still, she stayed alert, her heart beating in rhythm with her child’s soft movements.

Just as dusk fell and she began to wonder if she’d have to endure another night alone, there was a rustling at the edge of the clearing. Agatha tensed, breath catching, every sense sharpening as her eyes darted toward the door. The shadows shifted, and then—she saw her.

“Rio!” she cried out, her face contorting in pain as she felt something different twist inside her.

Sweat broke out across her forehead. She looked up feverishly, like some wild thing. Why hadn’t she come in? Why was her love’s cool hands not upon her, settling her, comforting her. Something was wrong.

Rio stood just at the threshold, caught between coming and going. Between life and death. Here she was.

Agatha’s eyes widened with realization and her hand flew to cover her mouth. “No. No!”

For the first time, Rio felt Agatha look upon her, not with love but horror. It was familiar. She'd known it was coming. It killed her all the same.

“Agatha,” Rio said, her voice unnaturaly cool and calm, “my love, it’s going to be alright. But I need you to trust me.”

Chapter End Notes

"Marching ever forward 'neath the wooded shrine
I stray not from the path, I hold death's hand in mine"

Capital A Angst, Capital H Hurt, Capital C Comfort (eventually)

Arrival

Chapter Summary

"You are not an accident where no one thought it through
The world has stood against us, made us mean to fight for you
And when we chose your name, we knew that you'd fight the power too"
- The Mother, Brandi Carlile

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Rio wished she had been able to come willingly. That she’d come to the cabin carrying an armful of flowers, taken her wife into her arms, and held her and supported her through the birth of their boy. They would’ve been so happy.

There had been one last soul to gather before she was to break off and rush to be with her love. But as she stepped out of the shadows onto the earth, she immediately realized where she had been called. And noose around her throat, she stepped off the gallows herself.

In doing so, she killed a part of herself. She buried the tears. She forced her face into a neutral expression. She bit the scar inside her cheek until she tasted blood with grim satisfaction. 

There were precious few moments to collect herself. Agatha, their child, they still needed her.

She wouldn’t break what they had built. She’d sooner break herself.

Rio opened the door, pausing as she saw Agatha. She was so beautiful. Even though she had been caught, she couldn’t yet make herself do what she must. Just another moment. One more selfishness. 

That ended as soon as Agatha, always too clever Agatha, looked upon her with realization. With fear, and horror. She’d never wanted her to look like that.

“No. No!” Agatha cried, half scrambling backward. For the first time she was willingly pulling away.

“Agatha,” Rio said, her voice unnaturaly cool and calm, “my love, it’s going to be alright. But I need you to trust me.”

“My love, please! You can’t take him! Please! He’s your son!” Agatha wept, cradling her stomach protectively even as her body twisted with another contraction.

Rio stepped towards her and knelt beside her. Agatha flinched and Rio wondered how many times in one day her heart could shatter.

“I know,” she said softly. “Please. Let me help you. There are still things I can do.”

Agatha looked at her warily, but instinct took over and she grabbed Rio’s hand in hers squeezing it tightly as she screamed again.

“Agatha, listen to me. I can give you time. I can give that to you both.” Rio whispered, brushing the hair from her face. “I can’t give you everything I promised. And I am so, so, sorry. But you’re going to have this baby. We’re going to do this together.”

Agatha held onto Rio’s hand with all the strength she had left, her breath ragged, her face a pure expression of pain and fear. Her mind was spinning, trying to reconcile the woman before her—the love who had promised to be by her side—with the looming shadow of death that had suddenly appeared, not for her but for their child. She gritted her teeth, every fiber of her being railing against what she knew Rio would eventually ask of her. 

“You’re not taking him from me,” Agatha said, her voice trembling, defiant even in her weakness.

“I’m not,” Rio said firmly, her own voice thick with barely concealed pain. “He’s yours, Agatha. I would never take him from you. But… he can’t stay here forever. We only have so much time.”

Agatha’s eyes blazed with a fierce protectiveness. “Then don’t take him from me. Don’t you dare. He needs us both, Rio. He deserves—he deserves a life!” She growled with the ferocious protectiveness that only mothers carry.

Rio felt her composure slip, felt the icy veneer crack. She wanted nothing more than to give her that life, to find some loophole, some way to rewrite Fate itself. But this story was already written. This was all she had. Every rule, every binding contract of her existence demanded this parting, even if it tore her apart.

“He will have that life, Agatha,” she promised, her voice choked. “It’s not everything I wanted to give him, to give you. But I’ll fight to give him as much time as I can.” She tightened her grip on Agatha’s hand, grounding them both in the here and now. “Right now, he needs you. He needs both of us. So let’s bring him into this world together.”

Another contraction seized Agatha, and she doubled over, squeezing Rio’s hand with desperate strength. Rio whispered words of comfort, soothing her, grounding her as best she could. She stayed by her side, offering soft reassurances, brushing the damp hair from Agatha’s face, pressing her forehead to hers.

Agatha bore down, her breaths coming in frantic gasps, her body arching with the effort. With every ounce of strength, she pushed. Rio murmured words of encouragement, a steady presence through the storm of pain. Time blurred, a rush of agony and endurance, until finally, a small, piercing cry filled the air.

Agatha’s tears spilled over, her breath catching as Rio placed their child—a tiny, fragile boy—into her arms. His skin was soft and warm, his eyes squeezed shut, and for a moment, every shadow, every threat, faded in the presence of their son’s first breaths.

“He’s perfect,” Agatha whispered, her voice trembling with awe and love.

Rio watched them, feeling a mixture of pride and despair twist within her. She reached out, lightly brushing her fingers over their son’s forehead. “He is,” she said, her voice barely a whisper. She couldn’t look away, drinking in the sight of Agatha holding their child, of this family she had never thought possible.

Agatha looked up at her, her expression softening as she seemed to understand the depth of Rio’s grief. She placed her free hand over Rio’s, squeezing gently, as if to tether her to this moment, to this reality.

“My love,” she whispered, her voice tender. “We’ll find a way, won’t we? For him. For us.”

Rio wanted to promise her that they would, to say she’d never let anything come between them. But the words caught in her throat. She felt the constraints of her power, the inevitability of what she was. The lie tasted bitter, so she said nothing, only brushing a kiss to Agatha’s forehead and cradling them both with a gentleness that betrayed every broken piece of her.

“I can’t stay here my love. I have to find someone else to take with me.” This was a cold certainty. There was a balance to maintain. A life for a life.

“Not him though. Right?” Agatha looked up still pleading, unable to allow herself to believe that they would escape this snare.

Rio blinked back tears and shook her head. “Not him. Not today. I will stay away. For as long as I possibly can.” The words shattered her. Yes, she would give up everything for them. “You understand what that means, right Agatha?”

Agatha felt her heart constrict, the weight of Rio’s words settling in. She held their son close, her fingers tightening instinctively around him as if to guard him from the truth that was slowly unfurling. She understood what Rio was telling her, what she was sacrificing. They could keep their child, safe and whole, but at a devastating cost.

She swallowed hard, her voice barely above a whisper. “It means… you can’t stay with us.”

Rio’s eyes softened, the grief in them cutting deep. She didn’t say anything, just brushed a gentle hand over Agatha’s cheek, her thumb catching a tear that Agatha hadn’t even realized had fallen.

Their baby cried between them and they both moved to soothe him. Agatha cradled him, Rio rocked them both.

“I can’t be here like this,” Rio whispered, her voice breaking on the words. “Not without… tipping the balance. This world—it needs life. And I… I am what comes after.”

Agatha’s heart twisted. She knew that it was true, had always known what Rio was, what her presence in the world meant. But to hear it now, with their son in her arms, felt like a wound she would never fully heal from. She pressed her lips to their son’s soft, downy head, inhaling his scent, memorizing every detail of this moment that would be a balm and a curse in the days to come.

“How am I supposed to… how am I supposed to go on without you?” she whispered, her voice raw, broken.

Rio’s hand tightened over hers. “You’ll have him. And he’ll have you,” she said, her voice filled with all the love and sorrow she could not hold back. “And I will be here, as much as I can. I’ll be in every breeze, every shadow, every night that passes.” She drew closer, brushing her lips over Agatha’s knuckles, her forehead resting against hers. “I know it’s not enough . It’s not what either of us wanted. My love, it’s all I have left to give.”

Agatha’s shoulders shook with the weight of it. She clung to Rio, needing to feel her warmth, needing to absorb her strength. She would do this, she would carry this burden of separation if it meant their son could live, could have a future. But the thought of living with only half of her heart, torn between worlds, was almost unbearable.

“No, it’s not enough,” she whispered, her voice thick. “But I’d rather live a life split in two than not have you in it at all. He’s yours, Rio. He’s ours.”

Rio closed her eyes, her own tears falling silently. She wanted to hold them both, to keep them safe forever. But she was Death, and Death did not belong in this world of summers and gentle mornings and new life. She cupped Agatha’s face, pressing a kiss to her forehead, letting the touch linger as if it could somehow imprint into her skin, stay with her always.

“Raise him to know who he is, Agatha. To know that he is loved, fiercely and without end. And tell him about us,” she murmured, her voice trembling, “about who we were, and what we had.”

Agatha nodded, the tears streaming down her face. “I’ll tell him every story,” she promised, her voice a choked whisper. “I’ll tell him about the way you look at me, the way you always made me feel safe, and loved, and whole. I’ll tell him about the garden. I’ll tell him about you… my beautiful, impossible wife.”

Rio’s breath hitched, and she pulled Agatha close, wrapping them both in an embrace that felt like the beginning and end of everything. They stayed like that, holding each other, memorizing every heartbeat, every shiver, every tear. 

Finally, Rio pulled back, her hand lingering over their son, her fingers tracing his tiny features with a tenderness that broke her. “You’ll be safe, little one,” she whispered, the words a promise, a vow. “And you’ll always know how loved you are.”

She looked back at Agatha, her gaze full of fierce, unyielding love. “I will come back,” she said, her voice soft but resolute. “Even if only in whispers, even if only in glimpses. I will find a way to be with you, both of you.”

And with a final, lingering kiss, she slipped from the cabin, leaving Agatha cradling their son, her heart torn in two.

Chapter End Notes

I didn't mean for these chapters to drop at such a heavy time. Sending so much love to you all. Please remember that this is a place where people like us will always find happiness even after the darkest moments.

Nicholas

Chapter Summary

Years pass, a little boy grows up. He brings his mother flowers.

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

“Mommy, look!” Nicholas ran to her on the porch, little fists full of flowers he had ripped from the earth with clumps of dirt still clinging to their roots.

Agatha paused, turning away from the clothes she was hanging on the line as he came bounding up to her. “Wow, what beautiful flowers! Let me see what you’ve got!”

Nicholas held his fists out, proudly showing her his bouquet. The sight made her heart ache and swell all at once—she knew Rio must have left them, carefully arranging them along his usual path through the woods. 

“Alright, my love,” Agatha said, crouching to his level, brushing some of the dirt off the stems. “Do you remember what they mean?”

Nicholas grinned, puffing up with pride. “The yellow one’s for friendship, right?” he said, holding up a wild yellow primrose.

She nodded, smiling as she brushed a stray dark curl from his forehead. He’d gotten Rio’s dark hair but carried her same thick and messy mane. “That’s right. What else?”

He carefully picked out a bluebell from his messy bundle, holding it up with a steady seriousness. “This one… this one’s for kindness?”

“Very close, love,” Agatha murmured, cupping his hand. “It’s actually for gratitude, for saying ‘thank you.’” She glanced at the flower, her own heart caught in her chest. “It’s the kind of flower someone leaves when they want you to know they’re grateful for something very special.”

Nicholas looked up at her, curiosity flashing in his bright blue eyes. “Grateful for me?” he asked, his voice softening with wonder.

Agatha’s heart squeezed, and she reached out to brush her hand over his hair. “Yes, my darling,” she whispered. “Very grateful for you. For all that you are.” She paused, her voice catching as she took his hand, guiding his gaze back to the flowers. “And for all the joy you bring.”

He seemed satisfied, returning his attention to the flowers and poking at the cluster left in his little fist. “What about this one?” He held up a dark purple hellebore, its petals delicate in his small hands. “Does this one mean anything too?”

Agatha hesitated, her eyes softening. Love and devotion, the flower whispered to her, and yet… there was something bittersweet in that meaning. The hellebore was also a sign of a promise and, sometimes, of a love that couldn’t stay close. She had taught him about it before, but she could see he had forgotten.

“It means love, and a promise,” she said quietly. “A promise that’s never broken, even when someone has to go away.”

Nicholas’s brows furrowed, and he held the flower close, turning it over thoughtfully. “Like… like when you tell me stories about Mami?”

Agatha felt her throat tighten, a lump forming that was both sweet and painful. “Yes, just like that.”

He gazed down at the hellebore for a long moment before a smile broke across his face. “Then I’ll keep it safe,” he declared, carefully tucking the flower behind his ear. “So she knows I love her back.”

Agatha’s hand trembled slightly as she reached out, resting her palm on his cheek, feeling the warmth and life of him, so real and solid and here. “Oh, she knows, my love. She knows very well.”

Nicholas beamed, his gaze lifting to the sky, where soft clouds drifted across the deepening blue. He reached for her hand, and Agatha held onto it tightly, her heart full of both the love and sorrow that was her constant state as they stood together on the porch.

For a moment, she swore she felt a gentle breeze curl around them, a soft warmth she knew was no mere coincidence. Rio’s love was here, just as present as the flowers their son held in his hand, lingering between the earth and sky, never far away.

True to her word, though it had been four years since she’d seen her wife, she never felt far from them.

Rio had found a way to remain close, like a hidden seam stitching their lives together. Even as years passed, Agatha saw her handiwork everywhere, in the small signs of love she left behind. Like gentle ghosts that guided and comforted.

Every spring, a patch of violets would bloom at the edge of the woods, even though they had never taken root there before. It was a small, quiet miracle—delicate purple flowers pushing up through the soil, as if Rio herself had coaxed them into life. Agatha would watch Nicholas find them, giggling as he picked handfuls to give to her, his eyes bright with a joy that felt like it had come directly from his other mother.

On stormy nights, when thunder cracked loud enough to make him jump in bed, Agatha would soothe him, murmuring promises that he was safe. And just as she did, the wind would soften, the harsh flashes of lightning dimming to a muted glow as if a hand had cupped the sky. She could feel Rio’s presence in that calm. A familiar gentleness wrapped around them both. Agatha had long since stopped wondering if it was a coincidence—she knew her wife’s touch when she felt it.

Even in the kitchen, Agatha found little hints of her love. On days that seemed endless, with chores piling up and Nicholas needing more attention than she had to give, she would open the pantry and find fresh herbs hanging from the rafters. Herbs that she hadn’t gathered herself. Sage and rosemary, lavender and thyme, all ready to be used in soothing teas and healing balms. Agatha would touch the leaves with reverence, her fingers brushing over the stems as she whispered her thanks.

And sometimes, on quiet mornings, she’d catch a whiff of something familiar—a scent she couldn’t quite place, but one that reminded her of Rio. It was like the smell of rain mixed with the rich, deep scent of earth. Petrichor, oak, and a touch of lily. She’d breathe it in, closing her eyes and feeling as though her love was standing just behind her, hands resting on her shoulders. She didn’t dare turn around, afraid the feeling would vanish like smoke.

Nicholas, too, seemed to sense these things. Once, after a winter snowstorm, they had gone out to play in the fresh drifts, and he’d spotted a trail of frost-covered footprints leading up to their front door and then disappearing into the woods. “Look, Mommy!” he’d exclaimed, his voice brimming with excitement. “Mami was here!”

Agatha had laughed, brushing the snow off his cheeks, but a part of her believed it. She could picture Rio’s steps in the snow, her lingering glance back toward the cabin before retreating into the safety of the shadows. Agatha had to wipe a tear from her cheek when Nicholas wasn’t looking.

On special days, like birthdays or the first day of spring, a single raven feather would appear on the windowsill. Agatha would find him proudly holding it, his fingers tracing its glossy surface with wonder. “For luck,” he’d say confidently, as though he knew exactly what it meant.

Agatha would tuck it into his book of pressed flowers and plants, where they kept a small collection of memories that Rio had quietly left for them. These were their keepsakes, small mementos that reminded them she was always watching, always keeping them safe in whatever way she could.

It was enough, sometimes, to feel her so close in these little ways. But on quiet nights, when Nicholas had long since fallen asleep, Agatha would sit alone by the fire and ache for her. She’d close her eyes and whisper into the darkness, hoping her voice could carry to wherever Rio was.

“I miss you,” she would say, her voice catching. “I love you. Always.”

And sometimes, in those rare, fragile moments, the fire would flicker, a gentle warmth would brush against her skin, and she’d feel it—the unmistakable, lingering presence of the woman she loved, like a promise of her eventual return.

One evening, just as Agatha was settling down by the fire, she heard small footsteps padding softly across the floor. Nicholas appeared at her side, his arms wrapped around his book of pressed flowers and feathers. His eyes were sleepy, but a spark of curiosity kept him awake.

“Mommy?” he murmured, clutching the book to his chest as he climbed into her lap. “Will you tell me a story about Mami?”

Agatha’s heart softened as she wrapped her arms around him, holding him close. She kissed the top of his head, breathing in the warm, familiar scent of him as she considered which story to tell. There were so many memories, so many pieces of their life she had tucked away, waiting for moments like this.

“Alright, let me think,” she said, leaning back and gazing into the fire, which flickered a little brighter as if it were waiting, too.

“Did I ever tell you about the time your mami taught me about shadeblooms?” she began, and Nicholas shook his head, eyes wide with anticipation.

“Shadeblooms? Like the ones in the garden?” he asked, a spark of recognition lighting up his face.

“Yes, exactly. But the first time I saw them was in a garden deep in the Underworld, in Mami’s special place,” she continued. “You see, they were her favorite flowers, but they only bloom in the dark. And just like her, they’re stubborn and resilient, thriving where nothing else will grow.”

Nicholas snuggled closer, his head resting against her chest as he listened.

“I was a little scared at first,” Agatha admitted, her voice low and warm, weaving the story just so. “The Underworld was full of shadows, and everything felt so still and quiet. But your mami... she held my hand, and she told me not to be afraid. She said that the shadeblooms could sense when someone was scared and that, if I stayed brave, they would bloom for me.”

Nicholas tilted his head up to look at her, his eyes shining. “Did they bloom?”

“Oh, yes,” Agatha replied, her smile softening with the memory. “She whispered something to them—something only she knew. And then, right before my eyes, the garden came to life. One by one, the blooms opened up, their petals so dark they were almost black, with tiny silver veins glowing like starlight.”

Nicholas’s mouth dropped open in wonder, and he hugged his book tightly to his chest.

Agatha chuckled softly, brushing her fingers through his hair. “Your mami always knew how to make the impossible seem ordinary. She could bring life where there was none, make flowers bloom in darkness. I think, even now, she’s still leaving pieces of herself for us, like those little flowers in the garden. She’s with us, even when we can’t see her.”

Nicholas was silent for a long moment, looking into the fire with a thoughtful expression. Finally, he looked up at her, his voice small and filled with hope. “Do you think she’s watching us now?”

Agatha took a deep breath, her heart aching with both love and the bittersweet distance that had become her constant companion. She wanted so desperately to believe that Rio could see them, that her love was woven into their lives in every small, quiet way.

“I know she is,” she whispered, her voice trembling as she hugged him close. “She’s always watching, always loving us. And whenever you feel something special—a breeze on your cheek, or a shadow moving just so, or even a feather by your window—know that it’s her, reminding us she’s here.”

Nicholas smiled, his little fingers reaching up to touch the raven feather tucked behind his ear. He leaned his head against her, comforted, his breaths slowing as he began to drift off to sleep.

Agatha stayed there with him, feeling the warmth of his small body against hers, and, for just a moment, she could almost sense Rio beside her, her love filling the room like a promise, unbroken even by time and distance.

The day after his sixth birthday, Nicholas asked her a question that froze her heart. “Mommy, what does ‘te veo’ mean?”

Agatha’s breath caught. She looked down at Nicholas, who was watching her with wide, curious eyes, completely unaware of the weight his innocent question had brought crashing down on her heart.

“Where… where did you hear that, love?” she managed, trying to keep her voice steady.

He gave her a small, lopsided smile, shrugging his shoulders as if it were the most natural thing in the world. He looked so much like his mother, Rio, in that moment. “I hear it sometimes when I’m in the woods. It’s like a whisper. ‘Te veo.’” He pronounced the words with delicate care, as though the phrase itself was fragile, something precious.

Agatha's heart ached with a mix of joy and sorrow. She knew those words as well as her own name. Rio had spoken them to her a thousand times, each with its own meaning— I see you, I love you, I am with you. Words they had spoken in shadows, in shared glances, and in moments too fragile to last.

Gently, she reached out, brushing a hand through his dark hair. “It means ‘I see you,’” she murmured, her voice thick with emotion. “It’s… a way of saying that someone cares deeply, that they’re watching over you. It’s something your mami and I used to say to each other.”

Nicholas tilted his head thoughtfully, looking back at her with that serious gaze of his. Far too serious for a six-year-old little boy. “So, if she says ‘te veo,’ it means she’s close, right? Even if we can’t see her?”

“Yes, love.” Agatha’s throat tightened. “Even if we can’t see her.”

Nicholas seemed satisfied with that answer, a small smile tugging at his lips as he leaned against her, content. She wrapped her arms around him, pressing a gentle kiss to the top of his head, as if her own embrace could somehow protect him from the sorrow she carried. He was so small, so full of hope, and she wanted more than anything to give him the certainty he longed for.

“Mommy?” he asked, his voice muffled as he snuggled against her.

“Yes, love?”

“Can we go to the garden tonight? The shadeblooms… they’re always open there. Maybe she’s there, too?”

Agatha hesitated, but only for a moment. “Alright,” she whispered, smiling despite the ache in her heart. “We’ll go to the garden.”

As night fell, Agatha led Nicholas out into the garden, where the shadeblooms glowed faintly in the moonlight, their petals unfolding like tiny stars against the deep shadows. Nicholas crouched down, touching one of the blooms reverently, as though it held a piece of magic meant just for him. In a way, she supposed it was.

The night air felt charged with something unseen, and Agatha closed her eyes, breathing it in. She could almost feel Rio there beside her, the sensation so strong it was as though Rio’s presence lingered just out of reach, wrapped in the night and shadows.

Nicholas looked up, a sense of awe lighting up his face. “I think she’s watching us,” he whispered, his voice filled with the kind of awe that only a child could muster.

Agatha knelt beside him, taking his hand in hers, and together, they sat in the silence of the garden, surrounded by flowers. The night seemed to hold them, wrapping them in a warmth that went beyond what was natural, something that Agatha knew, deep down, was a gift from her wife.

“Te veo,” Agatha murmured softly, hoping that wherever Rio was, the words would reach her.

For a moment, a warm breeze stirred the flowers, the petals shifting as though in answer, and a single petal drifted down, landing in Nicholas’s open hand. He looked at it, then at Agatha, his face breaking into a smile.

“I think… I think she sees us, too,” he said quietly, his voice filled with quiet certainty.

Agatha couldn’t bring herself to speak, her heart too full. So instead, she squeezed his hand, letting the silence speak for them, their love filling the space where words weren’t enough. And in that quiet moment, surrounded by the blooms Rio had left for them, she felt a fragile hope take root—a promise, growing quietly in the dark. She would never stop believing.

And she knew that her love would never give up.

From her distant vantage points in the shadows of the woods or on the other side of the veil, Rio watched Nicholas grow. Every milestone, every first word and step, drew her in. It compelled her to stay as close as she dared without breaking the delicate balance she’d sworn to uphold. And every time she saw them, she was filled with pride and a fierce protectiveness over both of them, a need to keep them safe that only grew stronger with each passing year.

She would carry this peace as long as she possibly could.

Whenever she saw Agatha teaching Nicholas, her heart swelled with admiration for her wife. Agatha’s patience, her gentleness, her relentless strength—qualities Rio had once witnessed only in fleeting glances and stolen moments—had flourished into something so powerful. 

Agatha’s love was a force that wrapped around their son. It shielded him in a way that only Agatha could. In those quiet moments, Rio’s longing to be beside them became a tangible ache, gnawing at her resolve, challenging her promises.

Sometimes, she couldn’t resist the urge to be near them, even just to leave a small token of her love where she knew they would find it. A feather, carefully laid on the windowsill; flowers arranged just so in the garden, mirroring the ones she and Agatha used to nurture together. These tiny gestures were all she could manage, but they were also her way of saying, I am here. I am with you.

And yet, the more she saw Nicholas and Agatha, the bolder she became in her attempts to catch a glimpse of them. On quiet nights, when the forest grew still, Rio would slip closer to their cabin, close enough to see the soft glow of candlelight in the windows, to hear the echoes of laughter or the faintest lullabies that Agatha would sing. 

Her love for them was both her solace and her torment. An ache that grew sharper each time she was reminded of all she was missing. She’d sit underneath their window for hours, just out of sight but always there.

One evening, as Rio lingered at the edge of the clearing, she caught sight of Nicholas wandering near the cabin. She held her breath, watching him curiously examine the berry bushes at the forest’s edge. His small fingers brushed over the not-yet-ripe fruit, and he looked around with an expression that was both thoughtful and eager. As though he were searching for something—or someone.

She took a quiet step back, careful not to disturb even the smallest twig, knowing she couldn’t risk being seen. But as she moved, Nicholas’s head snapped up, his gaze cutting through the shadows of the trees and meeting her own. For a heartbeat, neither of them moved, his eyes wide with surprise and something close to wonder.

Rio’s instinct was to melt back into the forest, to let the shadows swallow her whole. But the boy’s gaze held her in place, and she found herself rooted to the spot, unable to retreat. He took a tentative step forward, his face lighting up with a recognition that made her heart twist painfully.

“Are you…” he began, his voice soft and tentative, as if speaking too loudly might shatter the moment.

Her throat tightened, and she almost turned away, knowing how dangerous this was, how fragile the threads keeping her here had become. But Nicholas took another step forward, his eyes brimming with the same warmth, the same spark of life she had loved in Agatha.

“Yes,” she whispered, her voice barely a breath on the wind. “I’m here.”

A tentative smile spread across his face, and he reached out, as if trying to bridge the distance between them. “I knew you’d come,” he said, his voice full of a child’s quiet confidence. “I knew you wouldn’t stay gone forever.”

Rio wanted nothing more than to step forward, to pull him into her arms and hold him close. But she stopped herself, keeping her distance, her hands clenched tightly at her sides. She could feel the weight of all she couldn’t say, all the warnings and boundaries she’d imposed on herself.

“I’m… so proud of you, Nicky,” she murmured, her voice breaking just slightly. “You and your mother… you mean everything to me.”

He blinked, and his face softened with something she could only call understanding. He didn’t press her, didn’t move any closer. Instead, he gave her a small, reassuring nod. “We’ll wait for you, Mami,” he said, his voice full of certainty. “Whenever you’re ready.”

Rio’s heart swelled and broke all at once. “Nicholas,” she said seriously. “Don’t tell your mom you saw me.”

“How come?”

Rio swallowed, forcing herself to stay calm as she met his innocent, curious gaze. She crouched down, leveling her eyes with his, though she kept herself just within the shadows.

“Because…” She hesitated, trying to find words that would make sense, that wouldn’t hurt him. “Because it’s… hard for your mom to know I’m so close but still have to stay away. I don’t want her to worry. I want her to feel safe. And if she knew I’d been here…” She trailed off, knowing Agatha’s heart as well as her own, knowing the ache her presence would bring.

Nicholas frowned thoughtfully, the way he did when he was trying to work through one of the many mysteries his mother would guide him through. He nodded slowly, finally seeming to understand.

“Alright,” he said with a seriousness beyond his years. “I won’t say anything.”

Rio’s chest tightened, and for a fleeting moment, she allowed herself a small, bittersweet smile. He had inherited his mother’s strength, her resilience. Just as they’d hoped he might. She could see Agatha in the way he held himself, the way he met the world with a steady gaze and an open heart. And yet, he had her own quiet, watchful nature too—the willingness to understand even things that hurt, the way he seemed to look past the surface and into the shadows without fear.

“Thank you, Nicky.” She reached out a hand, and though she kept it hovering in the air between them, not daring to close the gap, she felt the warmth of his presence like a balm on her heart.

He smiled back at her, “I knew you’d come one day. Even if it was just to see us for a little while.”

“I’ll always be watching over you both,” she whispered, feeling the promise etch itself into her very being. “Every day.”

They stayed that way for a moment longer, gazing at each other with a quiet understanding that only deepened Rio’s resolve. She would keep her distance; she would continue to protect them both, no matter what it cost her. But this moment, this glimpse of her son’s love, would carry her through every aching night, every stolen glance from afar.

Finally, she stepped back, and Nicholas’s gaze never wavered, his small figure framed by the forest’s edge.

“Goodbye, Mami,” he said softly, his voice filled with more love than she thought she deserved.

“Goodbye, my heart,” she replied, her own voice breaking. “You know… you and your mom—you’re my greatest joy. You’re my light in all the darkness.”

Nicholas’s eyes shone, his little face filled with a mix of wonder and pride that made her ache even more. “We love you, Mami,” he said, his voice small but clear, unshakable. “I know you’ll come home someday. Even if we have to wait a long, long time.”

Rio bit her lip, fighting back tears. “You’re so brave, my sweet boy,” she whispered, her voice trembling. “I love you so so much. We both do.”

Nicholas nodded, smiling at her with such certainty it nearly undid her. “I know, Mami,” he whispered, “I feel you everywhere.”

Rio swallowed hard, feeling the weight of her heart splinter under his words. She nodded, unable to speak, knowing this moment would have to carry her for years to come.

As she took a step back, preparing herself to fade into the shadows, she heard his voice one last time, soft and full of love.

“Te veo, Mami.”

Her breath caught, and for one glorious, heartbreaking moment, she felt seen. She managed a trembling smile, whispering back just before she vanished, “Te veo, Nicky. Always.”

Chapter End Notes

all love y'all. hope you'll carry a little more angst with me.

A Walk In The Woods

Chapter Summary

The bill comes due.

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

The moonlight filtered softly through the trees, casting a silvery glow over the cabin where Agatha slept. The quiet of the night was thick, the entire world heavy with the weight of what was about to take place. 

Rio stood at the window, watching them—her family. She could see the gentle rise and fall of Agatha’s breath as she lay asleep, blissfully unaware. Nicholas was curled up in his bed, but Rio could feel his heart racing from where she stood, his dreams flickering with the same sense of unease that gnawed at her own chest.

It was time.

The ache of the decision tore at Rio, but there was nothing she could do. The time for waiting, for watching from the shadows, had passed.

Death had fought for a delay, but Fate and Nature were winning. She’d found herself intervening more and more frequently as Nicholas grew up and daring. He’d shared several brushes with her even though he had only spotted her the once.

One night he had slipped out from the cabin, and of course, Rio had followed him The moonlight guided her path, illuminating a small clearing where Nicholas had ventured out, captivated by the glow of fireflies dancing in the night.

Nicholas stood alone, his eyes wide with wonder as he reached out to touch a particularly bright firefly. Entranced, he didn’t notice the faint trail of smoke curling into the air behind him—a remnant of a nearby campfire that had been left unattended. The dry summer air was perfect for wildfires, and Rio knew the dangers all too well.

As the smoke began to thicken, swirling around Nicholas’s feet, he coughed, trying to shield his face. Panic flared in his chest as he took a step back, stumbling over a fallen log. Before he could regain his balance, a spark ignited on the ground, and flames began to lick hungrily at the edges of the clearing.

Rio acted swiftly, her movements fluid and precise. She extended a hand, and a gust of wind surged from her palm, extinguishing the flames before they could consume anything further. The smoke dissipated as quickly as it had appeared.

The fear in his eyes had broken her and she longed to pull him into her arms and console him. He’d hurried back and woken Agatha up, crying as he apologized to her. She'd soothed him gently, unable to scold him in this moment. Their son felt so deeply. Just like his mothers.

For seven years Rio had kept the wolves away. She’d isolated plagues, she’d chased off travelers who had stumbled across the cabin, she'd left herbs in the cupboards perfectly suited to cure any small ailment she sensed in him before it could twist into illness.

Eventually, though, it was not enough. She couldn’t keep away the multiplying threats. And she would not allow her child’s transition to be a painful or scary one.

Instead, she would try, to gently lead him by the hand. Just a little walk in the woods with his mother.

With slow, deliberate steps, Rio approached the door, pausing only for a heartbeat. She took in the scattered toys on the porch. One of Agatha's cloaks was draped over the banister and she lifted it to her face and inhaled deeply. She let a tear fall against the fabric. Rio considered taking it with her, but she was taking enough tonight. Instead, she folded it neatly and placed it on the chair. A lily of the valley bloomed between her fingers and she tucked it into the folds. I'm sorry. Forgive me. Then, with a flick of her fingers, the door creaked open.

She stepped into the room, quiet as a shadow, her presence a mere whisper in the stillness of the night. Nicholas stirred in his bed, sensing something, his small figure tensing. He blinked in the dim light, his gaze meeting Rio’s.

“Shh,” Rio whispered, pressing a finger to her lips as she knelt beside him. She wasn’t sure what hurt more—seeing him so close, so full of life, or knowing she was about to take him from the only world he’d ever known. 

Nicholas’s eyes were wide with a mix of confusion. “Mami?” he whispered, still groggy but recognizing her voice. “What’s happening?”

Rio swallowed, her heart twisting. “I was thinking… maybe you and I could take a little walk together,” she murmured, brushing a lock of hair from his forehead. “Just for a while.”

Nicholas hesitated, uncertainty clouding his gaze. He glanced over at Agatha, still asleep, her face softened in the peace of slumber. “Won’t she be sad? When she wakes up?” he asked, his voice small, unsure.

Rio’s heart clenched at the question, but she quickly masked the pain with a soft smile, brushing a stray lock of hair from Nicholas’s forehead. She leaned in, her voice gentle but firm, trying to soothe him as best she could.

“Of course, she’ll miss you, my love,” Rio murmured, her hand resting briefly on his cheek. “But she’ll be happy knowing you’re spending time with me, and that you’re safe. You know how much she loves you. This is just for a little while, and then we’ll all be together I promise.”

Nicholas’s brow furrowed, his small hand clutching the blanket tighter as though it might anchor him to the world he knew. Rio didn’t rush him. She allowed him his moment of hesitation and refused to let the sadness in her own chest rise to the surface. She had to be strong for him now. “Together like a family?” He questioned softly.

“Yeah honey,” Rio continued, her voice steady and comforting. “But first you’re going to be with me, and we’re going to have our own little adventures. Just like you and mommy have. But somewhere new.”

Nicholas looked at her, his expression still unsure, but the comfort in her words began to ease his worries. He nodded slowly as if he was still learning to trust in the promise of this new journey.

Rio smiled softly, though her chest tightened with every word she spoke. “You’ll love it, Nicky. We’ll go to places you've never seen. Maybe we'll even find some new flowers for your collection, the rare ones, the ones only I know about.” She paused, letting the idea hang in the air, watching the flicker of curiosity spark in his eyes.

“And I know you’ve always wanted to see the forest at night,” she added, her voice playful. “The stars are brighter, and the trees, they glow just a little when the moonlight touches them. You’ll see— it’ll be magical. Just like those stories your mama used to tell you. We’ll pass through there. Maybe we can wish on some fireflies! And then you’ll get to see where I live.”

Rio paused as she tried to build up some excitement in her voice though all she felt was dread. “There’s a huge city I built. And I have a great big dog. So big you can ride on his back as you explore!”

Nicholas’s expression softened, the lines of confusion beginning to fade as his imagination sparked to life. “That sounds... really cool, Mami. I like dogs. I think. But—“ He hesitated, glancing back at Agatha, then at Rio’s eyes, sensing there was something important that still felt off. “Will we stay there for a long time? Without mommy?”

Rio’s heart ached at the question, but she swallowed hard, forcing the promise from her lips. “Not forever, sweetheart. Just a little while. Just long enough to see some new things together. And when she’s ready, your mama will come to us. And we’ll be waiting for her. Just like you both have waited for me.”

The words felt like ash in her mouth, but she pushed them out anyway, doing her best to make them sound real. To make them sound hopeful. She couldn’t let him see the storm raging beneath the calm surface she was trying to maintain. Not now, not when he needed this to feel safe. She didn’t have it in her to take him against his will.

Nicholas’s eyes brightened again, reassured by her tone, though he still hesitated. “Okay. Just for a little while.” He relaxed slightly, curling against her side, the comforting warmth of her presence enough to calm his worries, for now. “Maybe we’ll find some magic flowers, like you said.”

Rio nodded, her breath catching for a moment as she kissed the top of his head. His hair smelled like his mother’s. She knew there was no magic flower that could ease the ache in her heart, but she would make sure he had these memories to carry with him. "I’m sure we will, Nicky. I’m sure we will.” 

She held him close for a moment longer, her mind racing with the weight of what was to come, but she forced it down, focusing only on him. When the time came, when he was older, she prayed he would understand— but for now, she would make it as easy as she could. What was death if not the promise of another adventure?

“Alright, little one,” Rio whispered, standing up carefully with him in her arms. “Let’s go see the stars.”

“Wait!” Nicky squirmed in her arms, hanging half out of them as he stretched to press a kiss to Agatha’s cheek.

Rio’s heart tore again, but she couldn’t stop herself from whispering, “Give her one from me too.”

Nicky did so.

And Rio carried him away with her into the night.

It was quiet. Just as she promised, the stars were bright. She knew that her son, despite his curious excitement, would be asleep long before they reached the door to her world.

He wouldn’t hear his mother’s piercing scream when she reached out to find an empty bed.

That was for the best.

She did not even allow herself to hope that Agatha would forgive her. She had kept her promise, she had given them both as much time as she possibly could. But she had broken them all the same.

Death takes and takes. There was no mercy or relief in it.

As they stepped through the door, the world grew cold.

Agatha in her sleep pulled the blankets tighter around herself against the chill. A temporary shroud. It wouldn’t last.

Nothing would.

Chapter End Notes

Consequences

Chapter Summary

The world shatters. A new character joins our play.

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Rio wasn’t sure what to expect as she carried Nicky through the door she had carved into her realm.

Normally she didn’t interact with the souls she collected. Her presence was usually enough, a magnetic force strong enough to draw them in. They passed as the mortals called it, and she didn’t think of them again until they arrived on the train as her workers. A few needed convincing. They floated above their bodies and tried to bargain with her. 

But always she had been able to maintain such a firm presence that they’d soon relented. Death didn’t make exceptions. She’d tried to make one and even that she had failed.

She took a shuddering breath, and pressed a gentle kiss to the top of her sleeping son’s head, unsure if he would be in her arms when she reached the other side.

Death stepped through the door, carrying the first soul in history that had come willingly.

And in doing so, unbeknownst to mortals or gods, a deep and powerful spell was cast.

Agatha woke slowly, lulled by the softness of the early dawn light filtering through the cabin window. She stretched, reaching out instinctively for the warmth of Nicholas’s small body beside her—but her fingers met only the cool, empty sheets. Frowning, she sat up, glancing around the dim room. “Nicky?” she called softly, expecting to see him somewhere nearby, his mischievous grin peeking out from behind the door or under the bed.

But there was nothing. Just silence.

A chill slipped through her as she called his name again, louder this time, her voice echoing in the quiet cabin. “Nicholas!” She threw back the blankets and hurried through each room, her heart pounding with an intensity that bordered on terror as every corner she turned showed no sign of him.

She flung open the front door, her eyes scanning the forest, expecting, hoping to see him wandering among the trees like he often did, perhaps collecting feathers or bits of bark for his growing collection. But the path was empty, the morning still. Too still.

Glancing around the porch, her eyes landed on her folded cloak, and the flower pressed against it. And that’s when it hit her as if a hand had clamped down on her heart. She was choking. She was drowning. She was dying.

She was screaming.

The realization came with a force that nearly knocked her to her knees. Her fingers dug into the doorframe as fury and grief rose within her, blazing like an uncontained fire. She staggered back inside, gripping the edge of the table, her breaths coming in harsh, ragged gasps.

She had trusted Rio, trusted that she would keep her distance, that she would respect the quiet life they had built here. The cruel irony twisted in her gut. The very woman she loved, the woman she would have given anything for, had come like a thief in the night and taken their son without a word, without even the chance for her to say goodbye. That was the cruelest part.

She had known, deep in her gut that the fragile peace she’d carried wouldn’t last. She hadn’t expected that part. But never in the worst versions of her nightmares had she imagined she wouldn’t be able to kiss that sweet boy’s cheek one last time.

A raw, guttural sound escaped her lips as she slammed her fist onto the table. “How could you,” she choked, her voice trembling with rage. “How dare you, Rio.” She shouted and her voice filled the cabin as if the woman could hear her. She hoped she could.

Images flooded her mind—of Nicholas laughing as he played, the way he’d tuck a flower behind his ear and say he was keeping it safe for his mami. The promise she’d made to him every night, that she’d be there when he woke up, that she’d keep him safe.

A sob tore from her throat as she dropped to her knees, hands curling against the floor. She wanted to scream, to tear apart the trees and drag Rio back to face her. To demand an explanation, to claw Nicholas from her grip if she had to. But there was nothing, only the empty cabin, the mocking silence, and the hollow ache inside her heart where her son had been.

Trembling, she pressed her hands to her face, her voice hoarse and broken. “You’ve taken everything from me. You’ve taken him… and I can’t… I can’t…” 

Her anger surged again, hotter and sharper than before. She swore under her breath, the words a promise, a curse. “I’m coming for him. I’ll bring him back, Rio. You may be Death itself, but you will not keep my son from me.”

Agatha rose, her grief hardening into cold resolve, her steps steady as she grabbed her cloak and stepped out into the dawn. The path before her was uncertain, the consequences unknown. It didn’t matter. She had a singular purpose now, one relentless drive: to find Rio and bring their son home.

Agatha stormed out of the cabin, her body thrumming with a raw, furious force. The soft light of dawn illuminated the garden, the delicate flowers Rio had left in secret for Nicholas, each bloom a quiet reminder of her love.

But now they felt like mockery.

With a snarl, Agatha dropped to her knees and wrenched a handful of lilies from the earth, dirt spilling from their torn roots. She crushed them in her fist, the petals bruising under her grip as her rage became uncontainable. Her hands moved with abandon, tearing through the flowerbeds that she had so carefully tended, shredding each petal and stem. Flowers that had once been symbols of memory and connection were scattered in pieces.

The garden wilted under her wrath. Each plant bent and curled, withering to gray before her eyes. She ripped out the roses by their roots, the thorns cutting into her palms without a second thought. She let the blood smear and spill. Agatha was no stranger to blood and thorns. Every part of her burned with a violent need to destroy—to unmake what they had built here, this small, fragile life forged under false promises.

With each plant she tore, the fury within her seemed to grow wild and consuming. It was a rage that defied words and surpassed any pain she’d ever known. Agatha was feral. She could feel her magic surging beneath her skin, responding to the dark, swirling storm inside her. The air around her grew heavy, thick with a palpable energy that sent a shudder through the earth beneath her feet.

Breathing hard, she stood and turned toward the cabin. Her home. Their home. The sanctuary where she had raised their son, where she had dreamed of safety, of love. The home she had shared with her wife .

But now, it was nothing more than a shell. It held only memories, promises broken and shattered beyond repair. It was an empty shrine to a life that had never been fully hers.

Her fingers curled, the energy pulsing through her hands, and she felt a sharp, almost exhilarating release as she flung her hands toward the cabin. The structure ignited instantly, flames licking hungrily at the wood, catching the rafters. The fire roared to life.

Agatha watched as the flames consumed everything, her chest heaving as her heart pounded in sync with the blaze. This was an ending, a severing of the ties that had bound her to this place.

No more would she be the woman who had planted flowers in the garden. The mother who hung laundry and told fantastic stories. That woman was dead. That woman had been buried under a mountain of grief the moment Rio had taken Nicholas away.

There was no softness left in her, no warmth. Only the cold, unyielding fury of a mother’s wrath.

As the flames climbed higher, reaching toward the sky, she stood rigid, her voice low and trembling with a terrible promise. “You’ve seen wars, Rio,” she murmured, her words swallowed by the inferno before her. “But you’ve never faced me. I’ll bring death to its knees if I have to. I’ll destroy everything for what you’ve done.”

The fire burned brighter, the cabin crumbling as the blaze devoured it whole. The morning itself seemed to darken as embers and ash swirled around her. And as she watched it fall, her face lit by the flames, she felt something solidify within her—a hardened resolve, a relentless purpose that would not be swayed.

She would find her son.

Agatha turned away, the fire blazing behind her, and with each step she took, she left the woman she had once been behind. This was war. She would stop at nothing until she had torn down the very walls of the underworld itself.

Life had been so gentle with—Agatha could not bear to think his name—with him. And her magic under that peace had slumbered. But now she could feel the familiar prickle under her skin. Love had been an interlude, vengeance was familiar.

Rio felt the first tremor ripple through the garden. She stood, her face impassive, as a rosebush suddenly wilted in front of her. Its vibrant blossoms faded to gray, shriveling under an unseen force. One by one, the blooms began to wither, as if crushed by an invisible hand, the leaves curling in on themselves, brittle and lifeless. They turned to ash and blew away.

It was like watching her heart being torn apart, petal by petal.

Around her, vines that had once draped gracefully in an arch over the stone bench—the bench they’d shared so many sweet whispers on—retracted and blackened. Their leaves curled and flaked to dust. The shadeblooms, delicate and aglow with ethereal light, dimmed and cracked, the soft petals falling away.

The entire garden—their sanctuary, their heart’s work, their love made physical—was unraveling before her eyes.

Rio forced herself to remain still, to keep her face an unreadable mask, but inside, a fissure ran deep, threatening to splinter her. She could feel Agatha’s fury, her raw pain, pulsing through each leaf that shriveled, each petal that was stripped from its stem. 

It was as if Agatha’s grief had found a way to claw into the underworld itself, carving a path to Rio’s heart.

Each withered bloom was a reminder of what she had done. Of what she had stolen, all for the sake of love—and, perhaps, for her own cowardice.

The vines twisted in agony, snapping and falling, leaving gaping holes in the canopy. As the last of life crumbled to dust, Rio closed her eyes, letting herself feel every ounce of Agatha’s anger, every unspoken word and howl of betrayal.

She deserved it. She had known what she would unleash, the damage it would do, but still, she had taken their son.

With a shaky breath, Rio turned away from the wreckage, her shoulders squared, her jaw set. She knew the destruction would be absolute, that Agatha’s fury would be relentless and unending until she found what had been taken from her.

Agatha’s wrath would be unforgiving, an unstoppable force against Rio’s immovable resolve.

And still, Rio held a fragile hope—that, one day, Agatha might come to understand. That, even after all the pain and ruin, she might find a way back to her, a way to heal what they had both broken.

She retained her composure until one last thing was taken. There was a tight pain on her finger, and she watched as the braided wooden ring splintered. Its wood cracked and twisted as if torn apart by an unseen force. Rio’s breath hitched as she felt the searing pain coil through her chest. She looked down, watching helplessly as the ring—the simple, beautiful piece Agatha had crafted from roots and vines that had once symbolized their unity—splintered into shards, each fragment falling lifelessly to the ground. 

The broken pieces lay in the dust, stark against the gray stone. Her fingers, now bare and aching, closed over the empty space on her hand as if trying to hold onto a part of it that still lingered. But there was nothing left. No remnant of their vow, nothing but the memory of that quiet moment when Agatha had kissed her hand and willed it onto her finger with a trembling smile.

Rio sank to her knees, her breath ragged as the full weight of the worlds above and below settled on her shoulders. She had always told herself that she could bear it, that she could withstand Agatha’s fury, even her hatred if it meant keeping their son safe. But this—this was agony.

The sound that tore from her chest was raw and unrestrained, the wail of a soul left in ruins. Her shoulders shook as her composure crumbled, her tears falling like rain over the lifeless garden. She pressed her hand to her chest as if to somehow hold back the tidal wave of grief and regret, but it was too late; the dam had broken.

Between sobs, she whispered Agatha’s name, over and over, like a prayer. It was a call to a lover she knew would not come, an apology too fractured to reach its destination. She was alone in the echoing silence of the underworld, her cries swallowed by the unyielding shadows.

She had faced countless battles and endured eons of isolation, but nothing had prepared her for the pain of losing Agatha’s love. This was not the wrath of an enemy she could conquer. This was the desolation of knowing she had been her own undoing.

She didn’t know how many minutes, hours, or years she spent here grieving her wife, her son, and their lives. Time was strange here. It also didn’t matter. She had been wrong before when she claimed that immortal beings couldn’t carry the same emotion for long.

Never again would she carry anything else.

When her tears finally subsided, Rio sat in silence, her head bowed, her fingers tracing the empty space on her hand as if she could somehow will the ring back into existence. She knew she would need to stand, to keep moving. There was always more work to do. But at this moment, she allowed herself to remain where she was, broken and bleeding, her heart as shattered as the garden that had once thrived under their love.

And with a voice worn raw from grief, she whispered one last time, “Agatha, please forgive me.”

The underworld was no longer a place of quiet shadows and solemnity. It was no longer cold and impartial.

It had transformed, as unrecognizable as the woman who ruled it. Heat seethed through every cavern and corridor, relentless and oppressive, baking the ground into harsh, cracked plains of metal and stone.

Smoke billowed into the air, turning it thick and suffocating. Rivers of molten metal replaced the cold, gentle streams, and the dark quiet of the underworld had been shattered by the clang of iron and the roar of machinery.

It blocked out any intrusive thoughts. It kept her mind sharply turned away from any memory of softness. Any memory of them.

Rio strode through this wasteland, her face set, her jaw clenched against the storm within her. She had ordered her workers to dismantle every hint of natural life, every reminder of what the underworld once was. 

The gardens had been the first to go, ripped out by their roots by Agatha, their memory replaced by the unforgiving walls of iron fortresses by her own hand. The trees that had once adorned her halls were now kindling, their remains fed to the ever-hungry furnaces that blazed and scorched every corner. The air was choked with ash, the ground covered in a thick layer of soot, and even the stones, once etched with the wisdom of ages, had been ground down, reduced to dust and nothingness.

This was no longer a kingdom of death, but a hellscape, brutal and unyielding. A prison she had built to cage her own heart. It was the only way she knew to protect herself, to suffocate any trace of tenderness, any memory of Agatha and their son, under iron and fire.

She kept herself busy, moving from one construction site to another, her commands sharp and unyielding. Workers toiled under her relentless gaze, their eyes downcast, their movements efficient and hurried. There was no softness left in her; she had turned into a force as brutal as the world she had forged around her, and they all felt it.

Yet, no matter how many layers of steel she wrapped around her heart, no matter how many memories she burned, there were moments when the ache resurfaced. She could feel Agatha’s absence like a wound that refused to heal, a pulsing, constant reminder of everything she had lost. An open sore that would never scar.

One day, while overseeing the construction of another wall of iron, she allowed herself a brief pause. The noise around her faded, and for a moment, the world fell silent. Her gaze drifted over the smog-ridden landscape, the fortress she had built from pain and bitterness, and she felt a flicker of something unfamiliar—a doubt, a faint whisper of regret that slipped through the cracks in her armor.

Her fingers brushed over the empty space on her hand, where the ring had once been. She clenched her fist, hard enough that her nails bit into her skin. She was queen of this barren, merciless kingdom now, her heart hardened and her mind unyielding. 

Yet, as she turned away from the endless expanse of smoke and iron, a single tear traced down her cheek, hidden and unseen, falling to the ground where it sizzled and disappeared into the scalding earth.

Faint footsteps echoed through the iron fortress as a young figure stepped into the hollowed-out halls, his face illuminated only by the flickering glow of molten metal in the distance. The boy’s eyes widened at the surreal world around him, but his gaze held steady, even as the shadows seemed to shift and twist. He could feel that he was being watched from every angle. This place—Death's dominion—was like nothing he’d ever seen, yet he stood resolute.

The Queen of this place appeared before him, a looming figure draped in dark armor, her eyes fierce, like embers flaring in the depths of an inferno. She observed him in silence, brow arched, bemusement masking the faintest hint of something far more dangerous.

"No mortal is allowed here," she murmured, her voice reverberating through the cold metal walls. She circled him slowly, inspecting this young interloper as one might a stray mouse who had wandered into a lion’s den. "I have not even granted safe passage to the most worthy of men. What makes you think you can pass through my gates without so much as a word?"

The boy swallowed hard, his fists clenched at his sides, but he didn’t back down. “I came here to find my brother. I know he’s here…somewhere.”

A smirk played on her lips as she took another step closer, looming over him. “You, a boy, have come into my realm to reclaim a life? Bold. Arrogant, even.” Her voice softened, dangerous with amusement. "Do you know the price of failure here? Or are you so ready to meet the same fate?"

For a moment, the boy faltered, but he gritted his teeth, raising his chin. “I don’t care what the price is. If I can bring him home…I'll pay it."

She let out a soft, mocking chuckle as if his determination was nothing more than a passing breeze to her. “And why should I humor such a request?” She let her fingers dance near him, as if ready to snuff out his life with a mere flick. “Few mortals have had the courage—or stupidity—to come here, and yet you stand, demanding my mercy. Convince me, then. Tell me why I should not simply crush you underfoot and be done with it.”

He opened his mouth, struggling to find words under the weight of her gaze. Finally, as she lifted her hand to strike he shouted, “Agatha sent me!” 

The name struck her like a blade. She froze the flicker of amusement in her eyes gone, replaced by something raw and jagged. The faintest tremor of disbelief crossed her face before she forced it into stone once more.

“Agatha,” she repeated slowly as if testing the word on her tongue, her voice now an unreadable murmur. “You carry her name as if it were a shield, boy. Do you even know what that name means?”

The boy’s breath was unsteady, but he stood his ground. “I know that she’s known you for a long time. I know that she doesn’t give up. And I know she wanted to help me find Tommy, even if it meant I had to face you. She believed I could do it.”

Her expression darkened, though something sorrowful flickered in her eyes, a memory of Agatha’s defiance and warmth that lingered in her soul like a scar. Of course, she'd sent him. But her hesitation was brief. Her face hardened once more as she leaned down, her voice cold. “Then prove your worth, if you think you’re brave enough to bear her will.” She straightened, the faintest smile curling her lips. “Let’s see how far you’ll go for this brother of yours. I hope my lovely wife prepared you well, boy.”
